<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Stout camphor tree genome fills gaps in understanding of flowering plant genome evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2019 5:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-01-09"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s), under exclusive licence to Springer Nature Limited"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="We present reference-quality genome assembly and annotation for the stout camphor tree (Cinnamomum kanehirae (Laurales, Lauraceae)), the first sequenced member of the Magnoliidae comprising four orders (Laurales, Magnoliales, Canellales and Piperales) and over 9,000 species. Phylogenomic analysis of 13 representative seed plant genomes indicates that magnoliid and eudicot lineages share more recent common ancestry than monocots. Two whole-genome duplication events were inferred within the magnoliid lineage: one before divergence of Laurales and Magnoliales and the other within the Lauraceae. Small-scale segmental duplications and tandem duplications also contributed to innovation in the evolutionary history of Cinnamomum. For example, expansion of the terpenoid synthase gene subfamilies within the Laurales spawned the diversity of Cinnamomum monoterpenes and sesquiterpenes. A high-quality reference genome of the stout camphor tree reveals its genome evolution and supports that magnoliid and eudicot lineages share more common ancestry relative to monocots."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>We present reference-quality genome assembly and annotation for the stout camphor tree (<i>Cinnamomum kanehirae</i> (Laurales, Lauraceae)), the first sequenced member of the Magnoliidae comprising four orders (Laurales, Magnoliales, Canellales and Piperales) and over 9,000 species. Phylogenomic analysis of 13 representative seed plant genomes indicates that magnoliid and eudicot lineages share more recent common ancestry than monocots. Two whole-genome duplication events were inferred within the magnoliid lineage: one before divergence of Laurales and Magnoliales and the other within the Lauraceae. Small-scale segmental duplications and tandem duplications also contributed to innovation in the evolutionary history of <i>Cinnamomum</i>. For example, expansion of the terpenoid synthase gene subfamilies within the Laurales spawned the diversity of <i>Cinnamomum</i> monoterpenes and sesquiterpenes.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>
                
                    <div class="c-pdf-button__container">
                        
                            
    
    <div class="c-pdf-download u-clear-both">
        <a href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0.pdf" class="c-pdf-download__link" data-article-pdf="true" data-readcube-pdf-url="true" data-test="download-pdf" data-draft-ignore="true" data-track="click" data-track-action="download pdf" data-track-label="link" data-track-external download>
            <span>Download PDF</span>
            <svg aria-hidden="true" focusable="false" width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xlink:href="#global-icon-download"/></svg>
        </a>
    </div>
    

                        
                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Aromatic medicinal plants have long been utilized as spices or curative agents throughout human history. In particular, many commercial essential oils are derived from flowering plants in the tree genus <i>Cinnamomum</i> L. (Lauraceae). For example, camphor, a bicyclic monoterpene ketone (C<sub>10</sub>H<sub>16</sub>O) that can be obtained from many members of this genus, has important industrial and pharmaceutical applications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Hamidpour, R., Hamidpour, S., Hamidpour, M. &amp; Shahlari, M. Camphor (Cinnamomum camphora), a traditional remedy with the history of treating several diseases. Int. J. Case Rep. Imag. 4, 86–89 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d10343e613">1</a></sup>. <i>Cinnamomum</i> includes approximately 250 species of evergreen aromatic trees belonging to Lauraceae (laurel family), which is an economically and ecologically important family that includes 2,850 species distributed mainly in tropical and subtropical regions of Asia and South America<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Christenhusz, M. J. M. &amp; Byng, J. W. The number of known plants species in the world and its annual increase. Phytotaxa 261, 201–217 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d10343e621">2</a></sup>. Among them, avocado (<i>Persea americana</i>), bay laurel (<i>Laurus nobilis</i>), camphor tree or camphor laurel (<i>Cinnamomum camphora</i>), cassia (<i>Cinnamomum cassia</i>) and cinnamon (including several <i>Cinnamomum</i> spp.) are important spice and fruit species. Lauraceae has traditionally been classified as one of the seven families of Laurales, which together with Canellales, Piperales and Magnoliales constitute the Magnoliidae (‘magnoliids’ informally).</p><p>The magnoliids (Magnoliidae), containing about 9,000 species, are characterized by three-merous flowers with diverse volatile secondary compounds, one-pored pollen and insect pollination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Palmer, J. D., Soltis, D. E. &amp; Chase, M. W. The plant tree of life: an overview and some points of view. Am. J. Bot. 91, 1437–1445 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d10343e643">3</a></sup>. Many magnoliids—such as custard apple (<i>Annonaceae</i>), nutmeg (<i>Myristica</i>), black pepper (<i>Piper nigrum</i>), magnolia and tulip tree (<i>Liriodendron tulipifera</i>)—produce economically important fruits, spices, essential oils, drugs, perfumes, timber and horticultural ornamentals. However, the phylogenetic position of magnoliids has been uncertain. They were considered to be (1) sister to the Chloranthaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Moore, M. J., Bell, C. D., Soltis, P. S. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Using plastid genome-scale data to resolve enigmatic relationships among basal angiosperms. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 104, 19363–19368 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d10343e660">4</a></sup>, (2) sister to the monocots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Endress, P. K. &amp; Doyle, J. A. Reconstructing the ancestral angiosperm flower and its initial specializations. Am. J. Bot. 96, 22–66 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d10343e664">5</a></sup>, (3) sister to the clade containing monocots and eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Qiu, Y.-L. et al. Angiosperm phylogeny inferred from sequences of four mitochondrial genes. J. Syst. Evol. 48, 391–425 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d10343e668">6</a></sup>, (4) sister to the clade composing Chloranthaceae and Ceratophyllaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Zhang, N., Zeng, L. P., Shan, H. Y. &amp; Ma, H. Highly conserved low-copy nuclear genes as effective markers for phylogenetic analyses in angiosperms. New Phytol. 195, 923–937 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d10343e672">7</a></sup>, or (5) sister to the clade including eudicots and Chloranthaceae–Ceratophyllaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d10343e676">8</a></sup>, based on plastid genes, plastomic inverted repeat regions, four mitochondrial genes, inflorescence and floral structures, and 59 conserved nuclear genes, respectively. Similar to the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group (APG) III system, the APG IV system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Byng, J. W. et al. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 181, 1–20 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d10343e680">9</a></sup> placed Magnoliidae and Chloranthaceae together as sister to a robust clade, including monocots and Ceratophyllales + eudicots. Furthermore, there are also unresolved questions about genome evolution within the Magnoliidae. Analysis of transcriptome sequences has implicated two rounds of genome duplication in the ancestry of <i>Persea</i> (Lauraceae) and one in the ancestry of <i>Liriodendron</i> (Magnoliaceae)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cui, L. et al. Widespread genome duplications throughout the history of flowering plants. Genome Res. 16, 738–749 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d10343e691">10</a></sup>, but the relative timing of these events remains ambiguous.</p><p><i>Cinnamomum kanehirae</i>, commonly known as the stout camphor tree (SCT), a name referring to its bulky, tall and strong trunk, is endemic to Taiwan and under threat of extinction. It has a restricted distribution in broadleaved forests in an elevational band between 450 and 1,200 m<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Liu, Y. C., Lu, F. Y. &amp; Ou, C. H. Trees of Taiwan. Monograph. Pub. 7, 105–131 (1988)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d10343e700">11</a></sup>. <i>Cinnamomum</i>, including SCT and six congeneric species, contributed to Taiwan’s position as the largest producer and exporter of camphor in the nineteenth century, and the value of their wood was further enhanced by their massive trunk diameters—the largest diameters among flowering plants of Taiwan—and their aromatic, decay-resistance quality that has been attributed to the essential oil <span class="u-small-caps">d</span>-terpinenol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Fujita, Y. Classification and phylogeny of the genus Cinnamomum viewed from the constituents of essential oils. Bot. Mag. Tokyo 80, 261–271 (1967)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d10343e710">12</a></sup>. <i>A</i><i>ntrodia cinnamomea</i> is a parasitic fungus that infects the trunks of SCT causing heart rot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Chang, T. T. &amp; Chou, W. N. Antrodia cinnamomea sp. nov. on Cinnamomum kanehirai in Taiwan. Mycol. Res. 99, 756–758 (1995)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d10343e720">13</a></sup>. The fungus produces several medicinal triterpenoids that impede the growth of liver cancer cells<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Wu, S. H., Ryvarden, L. &amp; Chang, T. T. Antrodia camphorata (“niu-chang-chih”), new combination of a medicinal fungus in Taiwan. Bot. Bull. Acad. Sinica 38, 273–275 (1997)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d10343e724">14</a></sup> and act as antioxidants that protect against atherosclerosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Hseu, Y. C., Chen, S. C., Yech, Y. J., Wang, L. &amp; Yang, H. L. Antioxidant activity of Antrodia camphorata on free radical-induced endothelial cell damage. J. Ethnopharmacol. 118, 237–245 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d10343e728">15</a></sup>. Owing to intensive deforestation in the past half century, followed by poor seed germination and illegal logging to cultivate the fungus, natural populations of SCT are fragmented and threatened<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Liao, P. C. et al. Historical spatial range expansion and a very recent bottleneck of Cinnamomum kanehirae Hay. (Lauraceae) in Taiwan inferred from nuclear genes. BMC Evol. Biol. 10, 124 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d10343e732">16</a></sup>.</p><p>Here, we report a chromosome-level genome assembly of SCT. Comparative analyses of the SCT genome with those of ten other angiosperms and two gymnosperms (ginkgo and Norway spruce) allow us to resolve the phylogenetic position of the magnoliids and shed new light on flowering plant genome evolution. Several gene families seem to be uniquely expanded in the SCT lineage, including the terpenoid synthase superfamily. Terpenoids play vital primary roles as photosynthetic pigments (carotenoids), electron carriers (plastoquinone and ubiquinone side chains) and regulators of plant growth (the phytohormone gibberellin and phytol side chain in chlorophyll)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zerbe, P. &amp; Bohlmann, J. Plant diterpene synthases: exploring modularity and metabolic diversity for bioengineering. Trends Biotechnol. 33, 419–428 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d10343e739">17</a></sup>. Specialized volatile or semi-volatile terpenoids are also important biological and ecological signals that protect plants against abiotic stress and promote beneficial biotic interactions above and below the ground with pollinators, pathogens, herbivorous insect and soil microorganisms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zerbe, P. &amp; Bohlmann, J. Plant diterpene synthases: exploring modularity and metabolic diversity for bioengineering. Trends Biotechnol. 33, 419–428 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d10343e743">17</a></sup>. Analyses of the SCT genome inform understanding of gene family evolution contributing to terpenoid biosynthesis, shed light on early events in flowering plant diversification and provide new insights into the demographic history of SCT with important implications for future conservation efforts.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Assembly and annotation of <i>C.</i> <i>kanehirae</i>
                        </h3><p>SCT is diploid (2n = 24; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">1a</a>) with an estimated genome size of 823.7 ± 58.2 Mb/1 C (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">1b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">2</a>). We produced an assembly derived solely from 85× PacBio long reads (read N50 = 11.1 kb; contig N50 = 0.9 Mb) spanning 728.3 Mb. The consensus sequences of the assembly were corrected using 141× Illumina reads and further scaffolded with 207× ‘Chicago’ reconstituted chromatin and 204× Hi-C paired-end reads using the HiRise pipeline (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">3</a>). A final, integrated assembly of 730.7 Mb was produced in 2,153 scaffolds, comprising 91.3% of the flow cytometry genome size estimate. The final scaffold N50 was 50.4 Mb with more than 90% in 12 pseudomolecules (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">1</a>), presumably corresponding to the 12 SCT chromosomes.</p><p>Using a combination of reference plant protein homology support and transcriptome sequencing derived from various tissues (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">1c</a> and Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">2</a>) and ab initio gene prediction, 27,899 protein-coding gene models were annotated using the MAKER2 pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Holt, C. &amp; Yandell, M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 491 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d10343e790">18</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">1</a>). Of these, 93.7% were found to be homologous to proteins in the TrEMBL database and 50% could be assigned Gene Ontology terms using eggNOG-mapper<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Huerta-Cepas, J. et al. Fast genome-wide functional annotation through orthology assignment by eggNOG-Mapper. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 2115–2122 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d10343e797">19</a></sup>. The proteome was estimated to be at least 89% complete based on BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d10343e802">20</a></sup> (benchmarking universal single-copy orthologs) assessment, which is comparable to other sequenced plant species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">1</a>). Orthofinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d10343e809">21</a></sup> clustering of SCT gene models with those from 12 diverse seed plant genomes yielded 20,658 orthologous groups (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM3">3</a>). 24,148 SCT genes (86.56%) were part of orthologous groups with orthologues from at least one other plant species. 3,744 gene models were not orthologous to others, and only 210 genes were part of the 48 SCT-specific orthologous groups. Altogether, they suggest that the phenotypic diversification in magnoliids may be fuelled by de novo birth of species-specific genes and expansion of existing gene families.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome characterization</h3><p>We identified 3,950,027 biallelic heterozygous sites in the SCT genome, corresponding to an average heterozygosity of 0.54% (one heterozygous single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) per 185 bp). The alternative (non-reference) allele frequencies at these sites had a major peak around 50% consistent with the fact that SCT is diploid with no evidence for recent aneuploidy (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">4</a>). The spatial distribution of heterozygous sites was highly variable, with 23.9% of the genome exhibiting less than 1 SNP locus per kb compared to 10% of the genome with at least 12.6 SNP loci per kb. Runs of homozygosity regions appeared to be distributed randomly across SCT chromosomes, reaching a maximum of 20.2 Mb in scaffold 11 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig1">1a</a>). Such long runs of homozygosity regions have equal sequence coverage than the rest of the genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">5</a>) and may be associated with selective sweeps, inbreeding or recent population bottlenecks. Genes located in these runs of homozygosity regions were found to be enriched in lignin biosynthetic process and galactose metabolism (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM3">4</a>), which suggest some potential roles in the formation of lignin–carbohydrate complexes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Azuma, J.-I. &amp; Tetsuo, K. Lignin–carbohydrate complexes from various sources. Methods Enzymol. 161, 12–18 (1988)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d10343e836">22</a></sup>. Pairwise sequentially Markovian coalescent<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Inference of human population history from individual whole-genome sequences. Nature 475, 493–496 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d10343e841">23</a></sup> (PSMC) analysis based on heterozygous SNP densities implicated a continuous reduction of effective population size over the past 9 million years (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig1">1b</a>), with a possible bottleneck coincident with the mid-Pleistocene climatic shift 0.9 million years ago (Ma). Such patterns may reflect a complex population history of SCT associated with the geological history of Taiwan, including uplift and formation of the island in the late Miocene (9 Ma) followed by mountain building 5–6 Ma, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Sibuet, J.-C. &amp; Hsu, S.-K. How was Taiwan created? Tectonophysics 379, 159–181 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d10343e848">24</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="SCT genome heterozygosity."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: SCT genome heterozygosity.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0337-0/MediaObjects/41477_2018_337_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0337-0/MediaObjects/41477_2018_337_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="671" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Number of heterozygous biallelic SNPs per 100-kb non-overlapping windows is plotted along the largest 12 scaffolds. Indels were excluded. <b>b</b>, The history of effective population size was inferred using the PSMC method. One hundred bootstraps were performed and the margins are shown in light red. <b>c</b>, For every non-overlapping 100-kb window, the distribution is shown from top to bottom: gene density (percentage of nucleotides with predicted model), transcriptome (percentage of nucleotides with evidence of transcriptome mapping) and three different classes of repetitive sequences (percentage of nucleotides with transposable element annotation). The red T letter denotes the presence of a telomeric repeat cluster at the scaffold end. LINE, long interspersed nuclear element. </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Transposable elements and interspersed repeats made up 48% of the genome assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">5</a>). The majority of the transposable elements belonged to long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons (25.53%), followed by DNA transposable elements (12.67%). Among the LTRs, 40.75% and 23.88% retrotransposons belonged to Ty3/Gypsy and Ty1/Copia, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">5</a>). Phylogeny of the reverse transcriptase domain showed that the majority of Ty3/Gypsy copies formed a distinct clade (20,092 copies), presumably as a result of recent expansion and proliferation, whereas Ty1/Copia elements were grouped into two sister clades (7,229 and 2,950 copies) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">6</a>). With the exception of two scaffolds, both Ty3/Gypsy and Ty1/Copia LTR transposable elements were clustered within the pericentromeric centres of the 12 largest scaffolds (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig1">1c</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">7</a>). In addition, the LTR-enriched regions (defined by 100 kb with an excess of 50% comprising LTR class transposable elements) had on average 35% greater coverage than the rest of the genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig1">1c</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">8</a>), suggesting that these repeats were collapsed in the assembly and may have contributed to the differences in flow cytometry and k-mer genome size estimates. The coding sequence content of SCT is similar to the other angiosperm genomes included in our analyses (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">1</a>), whereas introns are slightly longer in SCT owing to a higher density of transposable elements (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.001, Wilcoxon rank-sum test; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">9</a>).</p><p>As has been described for other plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Dong, P. F. et al. 3D chromatin architecture of large plant genomes determined by local A/B compartments. Mol. Plant 10, 1497–1509 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d10343e917">25</a></sup>, the chromosome-level scaffolds of SCT exhibit low protein-coding gene density and high transposable element density in the centres of chromosomes, and increased gene density towards the chromosome ends (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig1">1c</a>). We identified clusters of a putative subtelomere heptamer, TTTAGGG, extending as long as 2,547 copies, which implicate telomeric repeats in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Watson, J. M. &amp; Riha, K. Comparative biology of telomeres: where plants stand. FEBS Lett. 584, 3752–3759 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d10343e924">26</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">6</a>). In addition, 687 kb of nuclear plastid DNA-like sequences (NUPTs), averaging around 202.8 bp, were uncovered (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">7</a>). SCT NUPTs were overwhelmingly dominated by short fragments, with 96% of the identified NUPTs less than 500 bp (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">8</a>). The longest NUPT is ~20 kb in length and syntenic with 99.7% identity to a portion of the SCT plastome that contains seven protein-coding and five tRNA genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">10</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Phylogenomic placement of <i>C.</i> <i>kanehirae</i> sister to eudicots</h3><p>To resolve the long-standing debate over the phylogenetic placement of magnoliids relative to other major flowering plant lineages, we constructed a phylogenetic tree based on 211 strictly single-copy orthologue sets (that is, one and only one homologue in all species) identified through OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d10343e956">21</a></sup> gene family circumscription of all gene models from the SCT and 12 other seed plant genomes (see Methods). A single species tree was recovered through maximum likelihood analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d10343e960">27</a></sup> of a concatenated supermatrix of the single-copy gene alignments and coalescent-based analysis using the 211 gene trees<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Mirarab, S. &amp; Warnow, T. ASTRAL-II: coalescent-based species tree estimation with many hundreds of taxa and thousands of genes. Bioinformatics 31, i44–i52 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d10343e964">28</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig2">2</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">11</a>). SCT, representing the magnoliid lineage, was placed as sister to the eudicot clade (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig2">2</a>). This topology remained robust when we included a transcriptome data set of an additional 22 species of magnoliids order from the 1,000 plants initiative<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Matasci, N. et al. Data access for the 1,000 Plants (1KP) project. Gigascience 3, 17 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d10343e978">29</a></sup> (1KP), although lower bootstrap support was obtained (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">12</a>). Using MCMCtree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d10343e985">30</a></sup> with fossil calibrations, we calculated a 95% confidence interval for the time of divergence between magnoliids and eudicots to be 136.0–209.4 Ma (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig2">2</a>), which overlaps with two other recent estimates (114.8–164.1 Ma<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Massoni, J., Couvreur, T. L. &amp; Sauquet, H. Five major shifts of diversification through the long evolutionary history of Magnoliidae (angiosperms). BMC Evol. Biol. 15, 49 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d10343e992">31</a></sup> and 118.9–149.9 Ma<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Morris, J. L. et al. The timescale of early land plant evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E2274–E2283 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d10343e997">32</a></sup>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="A species tree on the basis of 211 single-copy orthologues from 13 plant species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: A species tree on the basis of 211 single-copy orthologues from 13 plant species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0337-0/MediaObjects/41477_2018_337_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0337-0/MediaObjects/41477_2018_337_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="481" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Gene family expansion and contraction are denoted in the numbers next to the plus and minus signs, respectively. The green numbers in the brackets denote divergence time estimates. All nodes’ bootstrap support was 100 unless stated otherwise.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Synteny analysis/whole-genome duplication</h3><p>Previous investigations of expressed sequence tags data inferred a genome-wide duplication within the magnoliids before the divergence of the Magnoliales and Laurales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cui, L. et al. Widespread genome duplications throughout the history of flowering plants. Genome Res. 16, 738–749 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1028">10</a></sup>, but synteny-based testing of this hypothesis has not been possible without an assembled magnoliid genome. A total of 16,498 gene pairs were identified in 992 syntenic blocks comprising 72.7% of the SCT genome assembly. Of these intragenomic syntenic blocks, 72.3% were found to be syntenic to more than one location on the genome, suggesting that more than one whole-genome duplication (WGD) occurred in the ancestry of SCT (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig3">3a</a>). Two rounds of ancient WGD were implicated by extensive synteny between pairs of chromosomal regions and significant but less syntenic pairing of each region with two additional genomic segments (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">13</a>). Synteny blocks of SCT’s 12 largest scaffolds were assigned to five clusters that may correspond to pre-WGD ancestral chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig3">3a</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Evolutionary analysis of the SCT genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Evolutionary analysis of the SCT genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0337-0/MediaObjects/41477_2018_337_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0337-0/MediaObjects/41477_2018_337_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="277" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Schematic representation of the intragenomic relationship among the 637 synteny blocks in the SCT genome. Synteny blocks (denoted by peach blocks) were assigned unambiguously into five linkage clusters representing ancient karyotypes and are colour coded. Purple blocks denote the synteny block assigned in the first linkage group (see also Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">13</a>). <b>b</b>, Schematic representation of the first linkage group within the SCT genome and their corresponding relationship in <i>A.</i> <i>trichopoda</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>Amborella trichopoda</i> is the sole species representing the sister lineage to all other extant angiosperms and it has no evidence of WGD since divergence from the last common ancestor extant flowering plant lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Zhong, B. J. &amp; Betancur-R, R. Expanded taxonomic sampling coupled with gene genealogy interrogation provides unambiguous resolution for the evolutionary root of angiosperms. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 3154–3161 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1087">33</a></sup>. To confirm that two rounds of WGD took place in the ancestry of SCT after divergence of lineages leading to SCT and <i>A.</i> <i>trichopoda</i>, we assessed synteny between the two genomes. Consistent with our hypothesis, one to four segments of the SCT genome were aligned to a single region in the <i>A.</i> <i>trichopoda</i> genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig3">3b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">14</a>).</p><p>To more precisely infer the timing of the two rounds of WGD evident in the SCT genome, intragenomic and interspecies homologue Ks (synonymous substitutions per synonymous site) distributions were estimated. SCT intragenomic duplicates showed two peaks around 0.46 and 0.76 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig4">4a</a>), congruent with the two WGD events. Based on these two peaks, we were able to infer the karyotype evolution by organizing the clustered synteny blocks further into four groups presumably originating from one of the five pre-WGD chromosomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">15</a>). Comparison between <i>Aquilegia coerulea</i> (Ranunculales, a sister lineage to all other extant eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Zhong, B. J. &amp; Betancur-R, R. Expanded taxonomic sampling coupled with gene genealogy interrogation provides unambiguous resolution for the evolutionary root of angiosperms. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 3154–3161 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1122">33</a></sup>) and SCT orthologues revealed a prominent peak around Ks = 1.41 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig4">4a</a>), whereas the <i>Aquilegia</i> intragenomic duplicate was around Ks = 1, implicating independent WGDs following the divergence of lineages leading to SCT and <i>Aquilegia</i>. The availability of the transcriptome of 17 Laurales + Magnoliales from the 1KP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Matasci, N. et al. Data access for the 1,000 Plants (1KP) project. Gigascience 3, 17 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1136">29</a></sup> allowed us to test the hypothesized timing of the WGDs evident in the SCT genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1140">8</a></sup>. Ks distribution of five out of six available species from Lauraceae revealed two peaks (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig4">4b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">16</a>), as was seen in the SCT Ks distribution (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig4">4a</a>) and corresponding to two synteny-based inferences of WGDs in the ancestry of SCT (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">15</a>). Only one Ks peak was observed in the remaining Laurales and Magnoliales species, suggesting only one WGD event occurred in the ancestry of these species (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">17</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">18</a>). The Ks peak seen in <i>Aquilegia</i> data is probably attributable to WGD within the Ranunculales well after the divergence of eudicots and magnoliids (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig4">4a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Density plots of synonymous substitutions (Ks) of the SCT genome and other plant species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Density plots of synonymous substitutions (Ks) of the SCT genome and other plant species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0337-0/MediaObjects/41477_2018_337_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0337-0/MediaObjects/41477_2018_337_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="402" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Pairwise orthologue duplicates identified in synteny blocks within SCT, <i>A.</i> <i>coerulea</i> and between SCT and <i>A.</i> <i>coerulea</i>. <b>b</b>, Ks of intragenomic pairwise duplicates of the Lauraceae and the Magnoliales in the 1KP project<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Matasci, N. et al. Data access for the 1,000 Plants (1KP) project. Gigascience 3, 17 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1203">29</a></sup>. Dashed lines denote the two Ks peaks observed in SCT. Brown and grey lines denote SCT and other Lauraceae’s Ks distribution, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Specialization of the magnoliids proteome</h3><p>We sought to identify genes and protein domains specific to SCT by annotating protein family (Pfam) domains and assessing their distribution across the 13 seed plant genomes included in our phylogenomic analyses. Consistent with the observation that there were very few SCT-specific orthologous groups, principal component analysis of Pfam domain content clustered SCT with the monocots and eudicots, with the first two principal components separating gymnosperms and <i>A.</i> <i>trichopoda</i> from this group (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">19a</a>). There were considerable overlaps between SCT, eudicot and monocot species, suggesting significant functional diversification since these three lineages split. SCT also showed a significant enrichment and reduction of 111 and 34 protein domains compared to other plant species, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">19b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM3">9</a>). Gain of protein domains included the terpene synthase (TPS) carboxy-terminal domain involved in defence responses and the leucine-rich repeats (628 versus 334.4) in plant transpiration efficiency<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Lang, T. G. et al. Protein domain analysis of genomic sequence data reveals regulation of LRR related domains in plant transpiration in Ficus. PLoS ONE 9, e108719 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1240">34</a></sup>. Interestingly, we found that SCT possesses 21 copies of EIN3/EIN3-like (EIL) transcription factor, more than the previously reported maximum of 17 copies in the banana genome (<i>Musa acuminata</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Jourda, C. et al. Expansion of banana (Musa acuminata) gene families involved in ethylene biosynthesis and signalling after lineage-specific whole-genome duplications. New Phytol. 202, 986–1000 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1247">35</a></sup>. EILs initiate an ethylene signalling response by activating ethylene response factor (ERF), which we also found to be highly expanded in SCT (150 copies versus an average of 68.3 copies from nine species reported in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Jourda, C. et al. Expansion of banana (Musa acuminata) gene families involved in ethylene biosynthesis and signalling after lineage-specific whole-genome duplications. New Phytol. 202, 986–1000 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1251">35</a></sup>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">20</a>). ERF responds and positively modulates biosynthesis of phytohormonal signals, including ethylene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Gu, C. et al. Multiple regulatory roles of AP2/ERF transcription factor in angiosperm. Bot. Stud. 58, 6 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1258">36</a></sup>. Expression of ERF has been implicated in positively modulating plant development from fruit ripening<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Jourda, C. et al. Expansion of banana (Musa acuminata) gene families involved in ethylene biosynthesis and signalling after lineage-specific whole-genome duplications. New Phytol. 202, 986–1000 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1263">35</a></sup> to secondary growth in wood formation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Seyfferth, C. et al. Ethylene-related gene expression networks in wood formation. Front. Plant Sci. 9, 272 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1267">37</a></sup>, as well as in increased resistance to abiotic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Chen, T. et al. Expression of an alfalfa (Medicago sativa L.) ethylene response factor gene MsERF8 in tobacco plants enhances resistance to salinity. Mol. Biol. Rep. 39, 6067–6075 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1271">38</a></sup> or biotic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Wu, L., Zhang, Z., Zhang, H., Wang, X. C. &amp; Huang, R. Transcriptional modulation of ethylene response factor protein JERF3 in the oxidative stress response enhances tolerance of tobacco seedlings to salt, drought, and freezing. Plant Physiol. 148, 1953–1963 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1275">39</a></sup> factors. Thus, expansion of EILs in SCT may stimulate ERF, leading to various regulation of downstream effectors that result in traits specific to SCT.</p><p>We next assessed orthologous group expansions and contractions across the seed plant phylogeny (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig2">2</a>). Gene family size evolution was dynamic across the phylogeny, and the branch leading to SCT did not exhibit significantly different numbers of expansions and contractions. Enrichment of Gene Ontology terms revealed either different gene families sharing common functions or single-gene families undergoing large expansions (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM3">10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM3">11</a>). For example, expanded members of plant resistance (<i>R</i>) genes add up to ‘plant-type hypersensitive response’ (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM3">10</a>). By contrast, the enriched Gene Ontology terms from the contracted gene families of the SCT branch (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM3">11</a>) contain members of ABC transporters, indole-3-acetic acid-amido synthetase, xyloglucan endotransglucosylase/hydrolase and auxin-responsive protein, all of which are part of the ‘response to auxin’.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">
                           <i>R</i> genes</h3><p>The SCT genome annotation included 387 <i>R</i> gene models, 82% of which belong to nucleotide-binding site leucine-rich repeat (NBS-LRR) or coiled-coil NBS-LRR types. This result is consistent with a previous report that LRR is one of the most abundant protein domains in plants and it is highly likely that SCT is able to recognize and fight off pathogen products of avirulence (<i>Avr</i>) genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Dodds, P. N. et al. Direct protein interaction underlies gene-for-gene specificity and coevolution of the flax resistance genes and flax rust avirulence genes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 8888–8893 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1319">40</a></sup>. Among the sampled 13 genomes, SCT harbours the highest number of <i>R</i> genes among non-cultivated plants (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">21</a>). The phylogenetic tree constructed from 2,465 NBS domains also suggests that clades within the gene family have diversified independently within the eudicots, monocots and magnoliids. Interestingly, the most diverse SCT NBS gene clades were sister to depauperate eudicot NBS gene clades (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">22</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">
                           <i>TPS</i> gene family</h3><p>One of the most striking features of the SCT genome is the large number of <i>TPS</i> genes (Ck<i>TPS</i>). A total of 101 Ck<i>TPS</i> genes were predicted and annotated, the largest number for any other genome to date. By including a transcriptome data set of two more species from magnoliids (<i>P.</i> <i>americana</i> and <i>Saruma henryi</i>), phylogenetic analyses of <i>TPS</i> from 15 species placed Ck<i>TPS</i> genes among six of seven <i>TPS</i> gene subfamilies that have been described for seed plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Chen, F., Tholl, D., Bohlmann, J. &amp; Pichersky, E. The family of terpene synthases in plants: a mid-size family of genes for specialized metabolism that is highly diversified throughout the kingdom. Plant J. 66, 212–229 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1373">41</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Fig5">5</a>, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">23</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">28</a>). Ck<i>TPS</i> genes placed in the <i>TPS-c</i> (2) and <i>TPS-e</i> (5) subfamilies probably encode diterpene synthases, such as copalyl diphosphate synthase and <i>ent</i>-kaurene synthase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Martin, D. M., Faldt, J. &amp; Bohlmann, J. Functional characterization of nine Norway spruce TPS genes and evolution of gymnosperm terpene synthases of the TPS-d subfamily. Plant Physiol. 135, 1908–1927 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1403">42</a></sup>. These are key enzymes catalysing the formation of the 20-carbon isoprenoids (collectively termed diterpenoids; C20s), which were thought to be eudicot specific<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Chen, F., Tholl, D., Bohlmann, J. &amp; Pichersky, E. The family of terpene synthases in plants: a mid-size family of genes for specialized metabolism that is highly diversified throughout the kingdom. Plant J. 66, 212–229 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1407">41</a></sup> and serve primary functions such as regulating plant primary metabolism. The remaining 94-predicted Ck<i>TPS</i> genes probably encode the 10-carbon monoterpene (C10) synthases, 15-carbon sesquiterpene (C15) synthases and additional 20-carbon diterpene (C20) synthases (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#Tab1">1</a>). With 25 and 58 homologues, respectively, <i>TPS-a</i> and <i>TPS-b</i> subfamilies are most diverse in SCT, presumably contributing to the mass and mixed production of volatile C15s and C10s<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Cheng, S. S. et al. Chemical polymorphism and composition of leaf essential oils of Cinnamomum kanehirae using gas chromatography/mass spectrometry, cluster analysis, and principal component analysis. J. Wood Chem. Technol. 35, 207–219 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d10343e1424">43</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Phylogenetic placements of the 101 CkTPS genes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Phylogenetic placements of the 101 Ck<i>TPS</i> genes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0337-0/MediaObjects/41477_2018_337_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0337-0/MediaObjects/41477_2018_337_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="895" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The phylogenetic tree was constructed using putative or characterized <i>TPS</i> genes from 13 sequenced land plant genomes and two magnoliids with available transcriptomic data.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Numbers of <i>TPS</i> subfamilies in the 13 genomes and three transcriptomes of major seed plant lineages</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>It is noteworthy that the <i>TPS</i> gene tree resolved Lauraceae-specific <i>TPS</i> gene clades within the <i>TPS-a</i>, <i>TPS-b</i>, <i>TPS-f</i> and <i>TPS-g</i> gene subfamilies (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">23, </a><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">24,</a> <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">27</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">28</a>). This pattern of <i>TPS</i> gene duplication in a common ancestor of <i>Persea</i> and <i>Cinnamomum</i> and subsequent retention may indicate subfunctionalization or neofunctionalization of duplicated <i>TPS</i> genes within the Lauraceae. A magnoliids-specific subclade in the <i>TPS-a</i> subfamily was also identified in analyses, including more magnoliid <i>TPS</i> genes with characterized functions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">23</a>). Indeed, we detected positive selection in the Lauraceae-specific <i>TPS-f</i> -I and -II subclades, implying functional divergence (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">13</a>). Together, these data indicate increasing diversification of magnoliid <i>TPS</i> genes both before and after the origin of the Lauraceae.</p><p>Ck<i>TPS</i> genes are not uniformly distributed throughout the chromosomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">12</a>) and clustering of members from individual subfamilies was observed as tandem duplicates (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">29</a>). Seventy-six <i>TPS</i> genes were observed in the largest 12 scaffolds of SCT. Of those, 60.5% (46 copies) belonging to different subfamilies were found in the 0.5–15 Mb and 22.0–24.5 Mb region of scaffolds 7 and 10, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">29</a>). Scaffold 7 contains 29 Ck<i>TPS</i> genes belonging to several subfamilies, including all of the eight Ck<i>TPS-a</i>, 12 Ck<i>TPS-b</i>, five Ck<i>TPS-e</i> and three Ck<i>TPS-f</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">29</a>). By contrast, only two members of Ck<i>TPS-c</i> reside in scaffold 1. Twenty-four Ck<i>TPS</i> genes are located in other smaller scaffolds, 22 of which encode the subfamily TPS-b (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">24</a>). Some of these subfamilies located on scaffolds 7 and 10 are physically in proximity of each other (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">29</a>). For instance, 3 out of 11 TPS-b-Lau III subfamily members were located adjacent to 4 out of 11 TPS-b-Lau V subfamily (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">29</a>), whereas other subfamily members were found not in corresponding syntenic regions but elsewhere in the genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">30</a>). Genes belonging to this cluster were not grouped together in their corresponding subfamily phylogeny (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#MOESM1">30</a>), suggesting that their arrangement might have occurred more recently than the last WGD event.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>It is currently challenging to find wild SCT populations, making the conservation and basic study of this tree a priority. Camphor trees have been intensively logged since the nineteenth century, initially for hardwood properties and association with the fungus <i>A.</i> <i>cinnamomea</i>. The apparent runs of homozygosity have been observed due to anthropogenic selective pressures or inbreeding in several livestock<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Peripolli, E. et al. Runs of homozygosity: current knowledge and applications in livestock. Anim. Genet. 48, 255–271 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d10343e2892">44</a></sup>, although inbreeding as a result of recent population bottleneck may be a more likely explanation for SCT. Interestingly, continuous decline in effective population size was inferred since 9 Ma. These observations may reflect a complex population history of SCT and Taiwan itself after origination and mountain building of the island that occurred around late Miocene (9 Ma) and 5–6 Ma, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Sibuet, J.-C. &amp; Hsu, S.-K. How was Taiwan created? Tectonophysics 379, 159–181 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d10343e2896">24</a></sup>. The availability of the SCT genome will help the development of precise genetic monitoring and tree management for the survival of SCT’s natural populations.</p><p>Our phylogenomic analyses of 211 single-copy orthologues from 13 representative seed plant genomes, including the first magnoliid representative, SCT, resolve magnoliids to be closer to eudicots than to monocots. This result disagrees with APG IV’s resolution placing magnloliids as an outgroup to a clade containing monocots, Ceratophyllales and eudicots, but is in good agreement with a recent analysis of 59 orthologous nuclear genes based on transcriptome data of 26 seed plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d10343e2903">8</a></sup>. Unfortunately, no complete genomic data of either Chloranthaceae or Ceratophyllacae are currently available for further re-examining the relationships of these two taxa, magnoliids, monocots, eudicots and the Amborella–Nymphaeles–Austrobaileyales grade. However, the placement of SCT as a sister to the eudicots in our analysis has important implications for comparative genomic analyses of evolutionary innovations within the eudicots, which comprise ~75% of extant flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d10343e2907">8</a></sup>. Consistent to early isozyme analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Isozyme evidence for ancient polyploidy in primitive angiosperms. Syst. Bot. 15, 328–337 (1990)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d10343e2911">45</a></sup>, within the Lauraceae, we identified the timing of two rounds of independent WGD events that contributed to gene family expansions and innovations in pathogen, herbivore and mutualistic interactions. Large Ks peak ranges in the Laurales and Magnoliales from the 1KP transcriptome data set may be due to variation of synonymous substitution rates in the different lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Matasci, N. et al. Data access for the 1,000 Plants (1KP) project. Gigascience 3, 17 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d10343e2915">29</a></sup>. Complete genome assemblies for representatives of additional magnoliid lineages are needed to pinpoint the exact timing of these WGD events. The SCT genome will serve as an important reference outgroup for reconstructing the timing and nature of polyploidy events that gave rise to the hexaploid ancestor of all core eudicots (Pentapetalae)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Jiao, Y. et al. A genome triplication associated with early diversification of the core eudicots. Genome Biol. 13, R3 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d10343e2919">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Chanderbali, A. S., Berger, B. A., Howarth, D. G., Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Evolution of floral diversity: genomics, genes and gamma. Phil. Trans. R. Soc. 372, 20150509 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0337-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d10343e2922">47</a></sup>.</p><p>Gene tree topologies for each of the six angiosperm <i>TPS</i> subfamilies revealed diversification of <i>TPS</i> genes and gene function in the ancestry of SCT.
